The Impossibility of Incapacity for God and the Meaning of Power and Incapacity
Imam al-Haramayn provides a rational proof that God cannot have incapacity and defines power and incapacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that God cannot be incapable and the crucial distinction between power (as potential) and incapacity (as actual inability).

Core Idea
Power is about potential for action; incapacity is about actual failure.

Imam al-Haramayn presents a rational argument that God cannot be described as incapable, and then answers an objection that would seem to apply the same reasoning to God's power. This lesson unpacks the proof and the key distinction that resolves the apparent paradox.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall flow of the argument. Do not worry about details yet.
2
Understand the proof against incapacity
Trace the logic: if God were incapable, the incapacity would have to be eternal, but an eternal incapacity requires an eternal possible object, which is impossible because no possibles exist in pre-eternity.
Notice how the proof uses the concept of 'ma'juz 'anhu' (that which is incapable of being enacted).
3
Examine the objection
The objection claims that the same reasoning would deny God's power, since power also requires a possible object (maqdur) in pre-eternity.
This objection is a common parallel argument, so pay attention to how it is answered.
4
Grasp the response
The response distinguishes power from incapacity: power is a potential attribute (salahiyya) related to the soundness of future acts, while incapacity is an actual inability requiring failure in the present.
The key terms are 'قدرة' (power) and 'عجز' (incapacity) with their specific definitions.
5
Reflect on the distinction
Internalize that power does not require an existing object, but incapacity does. This is why God has eternal power but no incapacity.
This distinction is fundamental in Islamic theology regarding divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستدل إمام الحرمين وغيرُه على استحالة اتصاف الإله بالعجز : بأنَّهُ لو كان عاجزاً لكان عاجزاً بعجز قديم ؛ يعني : لاستحالة اتصافه بالحوادث ، والعجزُ القديم محالٌ ؛ لأنَّه يستدعي معجوزاً عنه ، والمعجوز عنه لا يكون إلا ممكناً ، ولا ممكن في الأزلِ ، فلا عجز في الأزل
[ معنى القدرة ومعنى العجز ]
لا يُقالُ : ما ذكرتُموه لازمٌ عليكم في إثبات قدرة أو قادرية أزلاً ؛ فإنَّ إثبات القدرةِ يستدعي مقدوراً ، والمقدور لا يكون إلا ممكناً ولا ممكن في الأزلِ ، فيلزم أن لا قدرة ولا قادرية في الأزلِ !
لأنا نقولُ : معنى القدرةِ : صفةٌ يتأتّى بها إيقاع الفعل ، ولا يلزمُ مِنَ الوصفِ بالقدرةِ وجود المقدور بها ، بل تأتي أنْ يفعل بها حيث يمكنُ الفعلُ ، والفعلُ أزلاً محال ، فتثبتُ القدرة الأزليَّةُ مُتعلّقةً بصحة الفعل فيما لا يزالُ ، فأمَّا العجزُ : فمعناه تعدُّرُ ما يحاولُ إيجاده ، فلا يثبتُ بمعنى الصلاحيَّةِ ؛ لأنَّ الصالح لأَنْ يَعجِزَ لا يكونُ عاجزاً في الحال، بل قادراً، فالعجزُ إذاً لا يكون إلا بالفعل ، لا بالصلاحية
Imam al-Haramayn and others argued for the impossibility of the attribute of incapacity for God: that if He were incapable, He would be incapable by an eternal incapacity, meaning because it is impossible for Him to be qualified by temporal events, and an eternal incapacity is impossible because it requires something incapable of being enacted (ma'juz 'anhu), and that which is incapable of being enacted is only possible, and there is no possible thing in pre-eternity, so there is no incapacity in pre-eternity. [Meaning of power and meaning of incapacity] It cannot be said: What you have mentioned is necessary for you in affirming power or powerfulness eternally; for affirming power requires something possible (maqdur), and the possible is only possible, and there is no possible in pre-eternity, so it would follow that there is no power and no powerfulness in pre-eternity! We say: The meaning of power is an attribute by which the performance of an act is possible, and it does not follow from the description with power the existence of the possible object (maqdur) with it; rather it comes that one acts with it where the act is possible, and the act in pre-eternity is impossible, so the eternal power is established as related to the soundness of the act in what is yet to come. As for incapacity: its meaning is the inability to bring about what one attempts, so it is not established in the sense of potentiality; because what is capable of being incapable is not incapable in the present, but rather capable. Thus, incapacity is only by actuality, not by potentiality.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage contains a rational proof and a theological clarification. First, it presents the proof from Imam al-Haramayn (and others) that God cannot be described as incapable ('ajz). The argument runs: if God were incapable, that incapacity would have to be eternal (because God is eternal and cannot acquire new temporal attributes). But an eternal incapacity is impossible because it would require an eternal object of inability (ma'juz 'anhu), which would have to be a possible thing (mumkin). However, in pre-eternity (azal) there is no possible thing—only God exists. Hence, no eternal incapacity exists, so God is not incapable.

Then a section header appears: "The meaning of power and the meaning of incapacity." The text then raises an objection: the same reasoning used against incapacity seems to apply against affirming eternal power (qudra) for God. If power requires a possible object (maqdur), and there is no possible object in pre-eternity, then no power can be affirmed eternally. The response distinguishes between power and incapacity. The meaning of power is not the actual existence of a possible object, but rather an attribute that makes the performance of an act feasible (yutatta' biha). Power is attributed to God eternally in relation to the soundness (sihha) of the act in future time (ma la yazalu). Incapacity, however, means the actual inability to bring about what one attempts (ta'addur). It cannot be understood as mere potentiality (salahiyya), because something that has the potential to be incapable is not actually incapable—it is actually capable. Therefore, incapacity requires actual failure, not just potential, so it cannot be attributed to God due to the impossibility of any act being impossible for Him.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power requires an actual object at the moment of attribution.
Correction: Power is an attribute that makes action possible; it does not require an actual object. It is related to the soundness of the act in the future.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the crucial difference between power (qudra) and incapacity ('ajz)?
A) Power is eternal while incapacity is temporal.
B) Power is a potential for action, while incapacity is actual inability.
C) Power requires a pre-existing object, while incapacity does not.
D) Incapacity can be eternal, but power cannot.

Key Takeaway
Power is a potential attribute; incapacity is an actual failure, so God has power eternally but never incapacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a moment of helplessness, do you truly believe that God is absolutely never incapable? How does that belief reshape your immediate response to that difficulty? Commit to one concrete action this week that reflects your trust in God's perfect power.
?
The passage says incapacity is actual failure, not potential. In your life, do you often treat your fears of failing as if they are actual failures? How can you distinguish between potential weakness and actual failure so that you don't live in despair? Write down one area where you need to stop assuming failure before you act.
?
If God's power is eternally related to the soundness of all future actions, what does that mean for your worries about tomorrow? Are you living as if God's power might falter? Identify one specific worry you carry and ask yourself: Does this worry contradict the truth that God's power is perfect and never fails? What will you do to replace that worry with reliance on Him?

Why Power Cannot Precede Its Object
Sanusi proves that power cannot temporally precede its object.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that power must be simultaneous with its object, not prior to it.

Core Idea
Power must coexist with its object; otherwise, absurdities follow.

Sanusi now turns to a subtle but crucial point: the relationship between divine power and its objects. Some might think that God's power can exist before what it brings about, like a builder's plan before the house. But Sanusi argues this is impossible. The following passage presents two concise arguments that any student of kalam must grasp to correctly understand how God's attributes relate to creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage once in Arabic, then the translation. Notice the concise, logical style. The proof is in two parts.
Pay attention to the terms 'qudra' (power) and 'maqdur' (object of power).
2
Understand the first argument
The first argument: if power precedes its object, it would cease to exist when the object appears. But then the object would be brought about by a non-existent power, which is absurd.
A power that does not exist cannot cause anything.
3
Understand the second argument
The second argument: if power ceases, its opposite 'ajz (incapacity) could exist. Then the same thing would be both an object of power and an object of incapacity — a contradiction.
Two opposite states cannot coexist in the same subject regarding the same thing.
4
Consider the implication
Reflect: This proof applies to both created and divine power. It establishes that God's power is simultaneous with His act of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عقيب زمن وجودِهِ، واستحالة بقائه زمنينِ. وإذا ثبت استحالةُ بقائها لزمَ مِنْ ذلك استحالة تقدُّمها؛ إذْ لو تقدَّمَتْ لعُدِمَتْ حالَ وجودِ المقدور، فيكون مقدوراً بقدرة معدومة، وذلك محال. وهو. وتقرير ذلكَ: أَنَّهُ إِذا عُدِمَتِ القدرة جازَ وجود ضدّها؛ العجزُ، فيلزم كونه مقدوراً حالَ وجودِ العجز، والعجز يستدعي معجوزاً عنه، فيقعُ الشيء في حالِ وقوعه مقدوراً معجوزاً عنه! وذلك محال.
Following the time of its existence, and the impossibility of its remaining for two moments. And if the impossibility of its remaining is established, then from that follows the impossibility of its preceding; because if it preceded, it would be non-existent at the time of the existence of the object of power, so the object would be by a non-existent power, and that is absurd. And its explanation is that when power ceases, it is possible for its opposite, incapacity, to exist, so it would necessitate that the thing is an object of power at the time of incapacity, and incapacity requires an object of incapacity, so the thing occurs in the state of its occurrence as both an object of power and an object of incapacity! And that is absurd.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage is a proof that power (qudra) cannot temporally precede the object of power (maqdur). The author gives two arguments. First: if power preceded its object, then at the moment the object exists, the power would be non-existent (since it ceased), so the object would be brought about by a non-existent power, which is impossible. Second: if power ceases, its opposite (incapacity) could arise, so the object would simultaneously be an object of power (by the previous power?) and an object of incapacity, which is contradictory. Thus power must be simultaneous with its object.

مقدور
محال
عجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think divine power can be 'stored' or exist before the act, like a potential in a created being.
Correction: The proof shows that power must be simultaneous with its object for both created and divine power; otherwise, absurdities arise.

Checkpoint
What is the second argument given against power preceding its object?
A) Power would be non-existent at the time of the object.
B) The object would be caused by both power and incapacity simultaneously.
C) Power cannot be created.
D) The object would require a different power.

Key Takeaway
Power must be simultaneous with its object; otherwise, we end up with logical absurdities like non-existent causes or contradictory attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that power must be simultaneous with its object—otherwise absurdities follow. Think about a situation in your life where you are trying to hold onto power over a past event (like a regret) or control a future outcome (like a worry). Are you operating as if your power can exist without its object? How does this truth free you from the illusion of past or future control? What concrete act of tawakkul can you perform today to align your understanding of power with this reality?
?
The proof shows that if power ceases, its opposite (incapacity) takes its place, resulting in a contradiction. Reflect: Are there areas in your life where you claim to have power but simultaneously demonstrate incapacity—like saying you are 'too busy' to pray, but somehow finding time for entertainment? How does the logic of this passage expose the inconsistency in your own claims? What change will you make tonight to resolve this contradiction?
?
Sanusi's argument forces you to admit that true power only exists in the moment of action, not before or after. Have you been living as if your past deeds (good or bad) define your current spiritual state, or as if future promises can substitute for present obedience? In what specific way has this lesson shown you that you are relying on a power that does not exist? Answer now: what is one action you can take in the next hour that reflects simultaneous power and its object?

The Muqtarah's Objection: Power Can Precede Its Object
The muqtarah argues that power can precede its object, using analogies and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the muqtarah's objection to the proof that power cannot precede its object, including his analogy with knowledge and other arguments.

Core Idea
Power, like knowledge, can precede its object without being a cause.

The muqtarah raises a thoughtful objection to the previous proof. He argues that if power is not a cause of its object, then it can exist before the object, cease, and be renewed. This lesson presents his argument and the analogies he uses to support it.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get an overall sense of the muqtarah's argument. Notice the use of analogies.
The passage is a single continuous objection.
2
Identify the core claim
The muqtarah claims that if power is not a cause (ʿilla) of its object, then it can precede the object. Find where he states this.
Look for the phrase 'فإذا لم يكنْ مِنْ حكمها وجود المقدور'.
3
Examine the analogy with knowledge
The muqtarah compares power's relation to its object to knowledge's relation to the known. He gives an example of knowing Zayd's future existence. Understand how this analogy supports his argument.
The analogy shows that a relation can exist even if the relatum ceases and is renewed.
4
Consider additional arguments
The muqtarah also mentions subjective experience (trembling hands) and the renewal of color as further evidence. Note how these reinforce the idea of a non-causal relation.
These arguments appeal to common sense and observable phenomena.
5
Reflect on the objection's strength
Think about whether the muqtarah's objection successfully undermines the previous proof. What assumptions does it rely on?
The objection depends on the view that power is not an effect (taʾthīr) but mere relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المقترح: (وهذا عندي فيه نظر؛ مِنْ حيثُ إِنَّ امتناع التقدُّم إذا لم يكن مأخوذاً إلا مِنْ حيثُ استحالة بقائها، فالقدرة في التحقيقِ ليست علة لوجودِ المقدور ولا مُؤثّرةً فيه، فإذا لم يكنْ مِنْ حكمها وجود المقدور فيجوز وجودها قبل وقوع المقدور وتُعدَمُ ويُوجَدُ مثلها، فالمقارنة متعلقة، والسابقة متعلقة. ويصح أنْ يُقالَ: كانَتْ تلك القدرة مُتعلّقةً بِهِ قبل عدمِها، ثمَّ انتفَتْ فانتفى تعلقها ووُجِدَ مثلها، وهذا كما لو علم إنسان وجود زيدٍ غداً وقتَ طلوع الفجرِ مثلاً بإنباء صادقٍ، ثم قدَّرْنا تجدُّدَ علمه بوجودِهِ في الوقتِ المعلومِ إلى حالة وجودِ المعلومِ في الوقت الذي أخبر عنه. فإنَّ المقارنَ مُتعلّق بالوجودِ، والسابق مُتعلّق بالوجودِ في الزمن المخصوص، فالمعلومُ مُتعلَّق لهما، وأحدهما مُتقدِّم والآخرُ مُتأخّرُ. ولو قُدْرَ وجود ضدّ العلمِ؛ مِنْ ذهول أو غفلةٍ أو جهل أو شكٍّ حال وجود المعلوم.. لكان مجهولاً بما قارنه، وقد كانَ متعلقاً لما سبق من العلم. فإِنْ نُظِرَ إلى أَنَّهُ غيرُ مُتعلَّقٍ للعلم السابق في حال الوجود.. فكذلكَ المقدورُ ليسَ مُتعلَّقاً للقدرة السابقة حالة الوجودِ، ولا يمنعُ هذا تقدم وجودها، لا سيَّما على قولِ مَنْ يرى أنَّها لا تُؤثر، وإِنَّما تتعلق بالمقدور تعلُّقاً لا على وجهِ التأثير، كما نقول في تعلقِ العلم بالمعلوم، فأيُّ شيءٍ يمنعُ مِنْ تعلَّقِ القدرةِ؟! حتى إِنَّ الإنسانَ يُحِسُّ مِنْ نفسِهِ تفرقةً قبل الفعل بين يديهِ في حالِ رعشتِه، وبين يديهِ في حال سلامتِهِ، وما ذاكَ إِلا أَنَّهُ وجدَ قبل الفعل صفةً متعلقة به، وإذا صح أنَّ اللون تتجدَّدُ أمثالُهُ .. فالقدرة أيضاً تتجدد أمثالها إلى حالة وجودِ المقدور، فتأمَّلوا ذلك يرحمْكُمُ اللهُ تعالى.
The objector said: (This, in my view, requires consideration; because the impossibility of precedence, if it is not taken except from the impossibility of its remaining, then power in reality is not a cause for the existence of the object nor an effect in it. So if it is not from its ruling that the object exists, then it is permissible for it to exist before the occurrence of the object, and it ceases and its like comes into existence. So the simultaneous is related, and the preceding is related. And it is valid to say: that power was related to it before its non-existence, then it ceased so its relationship ceased, and its like came into existence. And this is like if a person knows the existence of Zayd tomorrow at the time of dawn, for example, by a truthful report, then we imagine the renewal of his knowledge of his existence at the known time up to the state of the existence of the known at the time he reported about. For the simultaneous is related to existence, and the preceding is related to existence at a specific time, so the known is related to both, and one is prior and the other posterior. And if the opposite of knowledge were assumed—from absent-mindedness, negligence, ignorance, or doubt—at the time of the existence of the known, it would be unknown by what accompanies it, and it had been related to what preceded from knowledge. Then if it is considered that it is not related to the prior knowledge in the state of existence, then likewise the object is not related to the prior power in the state of existence, and this does not prevent the precedence of its existence, especially according to the one who holds that it does not effect, but rather is related to the object in a relation not in the way of effect, as we say regarding the relation of knowledge to the known. So what prevents the relation of power? Even if a man feels from himself a difference before the action between his hands in a state of trembling and between his hands in a state of health, and that is only because he found before the action a quality related to it. And if it is valid that color has recurring likenesses, then power also has recurring likenesses until the state of the existence of the object. So ponder that, may Allah have mercy on you.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage is an objection (iʿtirāḍ) to the previous proof. The objector argues that the proof's assumption that power cannot precede because it would cease is weak, because power is not a cause (ʿilla) of the object's existence. If power does not cause the object, then it can exist before the object, cease, and then a similar power can arise at the time of the object. He compares this to knowledge: a person can know that Zayd will exist tomorrow; that knowledge continues until the time of Zayd's existence. Even if the knowledge is replaced by forgetfulness at the moment of Zayd's existence, Zayd is still known by the prior knowledge. Similarly, power can be related to the object even if it ceases, as long as a similar power arises. The objector further argues that this is especially plausible for those who hold that power does not effect (lā tuʾaththir) but is merely a relation (taʿalluq). He also cites a subjective experience: a person senses a difference in his ability to act when his hands are trembling vs. healthy, indicating a preceding relation of power. Finally, he uses the analogy of color which is renewed in its instances, so power can also be renewed.

Key Terms
مقترح
قدرة
مقدور
تعلق
علم
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary analogy used by the muqtarah to argue that power can precede its object?
A) The relation of a cause to its effect.
B) The renewal of color in a substance.
C) The relation of knowledge to the known.
D) The feeling of trembling before an action.

Key Takeaway
The muqtarah's objection relies on the claim that power is not a cause, allowing it to precede its object, akin to knowledge preceding the known.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The muqtarah argues that power is not a cause but a relation. In your daily life, do you tend to see your own abilities as causes of actions, or as relations given by God? How would viewing your power as a non-causal gift change your reliance on Him?
?
Consider the analogy of knowledge: you know something will happen without causing it. Do you trust that God's power over your future actions is certain even when you feel weak or unable? What would it mean to live with that trust today?
?
The passage ends with 'ponder that, may Allah have mercy on you.' When was the last time you truly pondered the nature of God's attributes beyond theoretical discussion? What one step can you take to move from knowing this concept to letting it transform your worship?

Commentator's Conclusion: Created Power and Its Relation
Commentator's conclusion: proof for created power, not simultaneity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's conclusion that the proof for created power does not prove simultaneity with its object, and his personal inclination toward the view that power can precede its effect.

Core Idea
The proof establishes that we have power, not when it acts.

The commentator now evaluates the proof that was just presented. He clarifies its precise scope: it proves the existence of created power, but it does not prove that power must be simultaneous with its effect. He then shares his personal leaning toward the view that power can precede its object, using an analogy with prior knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once. Then read the literal English translation. Notice the commentator's careful phrasing: he states what the proof is for and what it is not for.
The passage is a short but dense commentary paragraph.
2
Identify the two claims
The commentator makes two main claims: (1) The proof demonstrates the existence of created power, refuting the Jabriyya who deny human power. (2) The proof does not demonstrate that power must be simultaneous with its object. Understand the difference.
The Jabriyya claim that only the object of power exists, not the power itself.
3
Understand the commentator's inclination
The commentator says 'the soul inclines to what the muqtarah mentioned.' The muqtarah argued that power can precede its object. Reflect on why the soul might find this view plausible.
The footnote analogy uses prior knowledge relating to a known object even after the knowledge ceases.
4
Reflect on the footnote analogy
In the footnote, the commentator analogizes power to knowledge: just as prior knowledge relates to its object after it is replaced, so prior power can relate to its object. Consider how this analogy supports the muqtarah's view.
This analogy is a common kalam argument about relational attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انتهى قوله: (لما نجدُهُ مِنَ الفرقِ الضروريّ...) إلى آخره: هذا دليل على وجودِ القدرةِ الحادثةِ وإنْ كانَتْ لا تُؤثر، ردّاً على الجبريَّةِ القائلين بنفيها، وأنَّ الموجودَ المقدورُ فقط، لا أَنَّهُ دليلٌ على مقارنة تلك القدرة للمقدورِ؛ فإنَّا لم نتعرّض في العقيدة لدليلها، ودليلها: ما قدمناه قبلُ، وذكرنا ما للمقترح فيهِ مِنَ النظرِ، والنفسُ أميل إلى ما ذكرَهُ المقترح، والله أعلم. (1) يعني: كان المعلوم متعلقاً لما سبق من العلم؛ أي: وكذلك القدرة؛ فإنها إذا عدمت وطرأ مثلها، ووجد المقدور مقارناً لذلك المثل.. لا يمنع ذلك من تعلق القدرة السابقة بذلك المقدور. (2) انظر (شرح الإرشاد) (ص 363).
His speech ends: ('Because we find from the necessary distinction...') to its end: This is a proof for the existence of created power even if it does not effect, as a refutation of the Jabriyya who deny it and claim that only the object of power exists, not that it is a proof for the simultaneity of that power with the object. For we did not address its proof in the creed; its proof is what we have presented before, and we have mentioned what the objector has to consider in it, and the soul inclines to what the objector mentioned, and Allah knows best. (1) Meaning: the known was related to what preceded from knowledge; i.e., likewise power: if it ceases and its like arises, and the object is found simultaneous with that like, that does not prevent the prior power's relation to that object. (2) See Sharḥ al-Irshād, p. 363.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage is the commentator's evaluation of the preceding exchange. He states that the earlier proof (probably the proof that power cannot precede) is actually a proof for the existence of created power (qudra ḥāditha) against the Jabriyya who deny human power altogether. It is not, as some might think, a proof that power must be simultaneous with its object. The commentator then acknowledges the muqtarah's objection, saying that the soul (al-nafs) inclines toward the muqtarah's view that power can precede. He concludes with 'Allah knows best' and provides a footnote explaining the analogy with knowledge: just as the prior knowledge relates to the known even after it is replaced, so the prior power relates to the object. The second footnote is a reference to Sharḥ al-Irshād.

Correction: The commentator explicitly states the proof establishes the existence of created human power against the Jabriyya, not simultaneity. It refutes those who deny human power altogether.

Proof That Power Distinguishes Voluntary from Involuntary Motion
Sanusi proves that the differentiating attribute is power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's logical argument that the attribute distinguishing voluntary from involuntary motion must be power, and how he eliminates other candidates.

Core Idea
Power is the only attribute that can differentiate voluntary from involuntary motion.

Sanusi presents a logical deduction to identify the attribute that separates voluntary from involuntary motion. He begins by narrowing down the type of attribute, then eliminates possibilities one by one, eventually concluding that only power fits the criteria. This passage demonstrates the rigorous analytical method of kalām.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Problem
Recognize that the issue is to find which attribute distinguishes voluntary motion (e.g., raising hand) from involuntary motion (e.g., shivering).
Voluntary motion involves choice, involuntary does not.
2
First Division: Accidents Needing Life vs. Not
Sanusi divides accidents into those requiring life and those that do not. He eliminates the latter because they have no relation to motion and are common to both types.
Colors, tastes, odors are examples of accidents that do not require life.
3
Eliminate Knowledge, Life, Speech
These attributes exist regardless of motion's presence or absence, so they cannot differentiate.
Knowledge remains even when motion is absent.
4
Eliminate Will
Differentiation occurs even during absent-mindedness when will is absent, so will cannot be the differentiator.
Absent-mindedness is a state where voluntary motion happens without conscious will.
5
Conclusion: Power
The only remaining candidate is an accident with a relation to motion, which Sanusi calls power. He notes the Mu'tazila agree it is related but differ on effectiveness.
Power is the attribute that enables action and distinguishes voluntary from involuntary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فتعيَّن أن تكون تلك الصفة عرضاً ، ثم لا يخلو : إمَّا أَنْ يكونَ ممَّا يُشترط في ثبوتِهِ الحياة أو لا، والثاني باطل ؛ لأنَّه لا تعلق لهُ بالحركة ؛ كالألوان والطعوم والروائح ، ولأنَّه مشترك بين الحركتين ، والمشترك بين شيئين لا يُفرَّقُ بهِ بينهما ، فتعين الأول ؛ وهو ما يُشترط في ثبوتِهِ الحياة . ثم يبطل كونه علماً أو حياةً أو كلاماً ؛ لوجودِ الكل مع ثبوتِ الحركتين ونفيهما ، ويبطل كونه إرادةً ؛ لوجودِ التفرقة بين الحركتين حالَ الذهول ، فتعيَّنَ أنْ يكونَ عَرَضاً له نسبة وتعلَّق ما بالحركة ؛ وهو الذي سميناه قدرةً ، وإن اختلفنا نحنُ والمعتزلة في أنَّها مِنَ الصفاتِ المؤثرة أم لا ، مع الاتفاق على أنَّها مِنَ الصفات المتعلقة
It was thus determined that that attribute is an accident. Then it is not free: either it is something whose existence requires life or not. The second is invalid because it has no relation to motion, like colors, tastes, and odors, and because it is common between the two motions, and what is common between two things does not differentiate between them. So the first is established, which is what requires life for its existence. Then it is invalid that it be knowledge, life, or speech, because all are present with both the existence and non-existence of the two motions. And it is invalid that it be will, because differentiation between the two motions occurs during absent-mindedness. So it was determined that it be an accident that has a relation and some connection to motion, and that is what we have named power (qudra), even though we and the Mu'tazila differ as to whether it is among the effective attributes or not, while agreeing that it is among the related attributes.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage is a proof. Sanusi argues that the attribute which distinguishes voluntary from involuntary motion must be an accident (ʿaraḍ). He first divides such accidents into those that require life for their existence and those that do not. He eliminates the second category because those accidents (like colors) have no relation to motion and are common to both types of motion, so they cannot differentiate. Thus the attribute must be one that requires life. Next, he eliminates knowledge, life, speech, and will. He shows that knowledge, life, and speech exist equally with both motions, so they cannot differentiate. Will cannot be the differentiator because differentiation occurs even when the agent is absent-minded (dhuhūl), and will is absent in that state. Therefore the attribute must be a relation-accident connected to motion, which he calls power (qudra). Sanusi notes that he and the Muʿtazila differ on whether power is effective (muʾaththira) but agree it is a related attribute (ṣifa mutaʿalliqa).

Key Terms
عرض (accident)
قدرة (power)
اختيار (choice)
اضطرار (compulsion)
حركة (motion)
ذهول (absent-mindedness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that will (irāda) is the attribute that differentiates voluntary from involuntary motion.
Correction: Sanusi shows that differentiation occurs even during absent-mindedness, when will is absent, so will cannot be the differentiator.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, which attribute differentiates voluntary from involuntary motion?
A) Knowledge (علم)
B) Speech (كلام)
C) Power (قدرة)
D) Life (حياة)

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's elimination argument demonstrates that power is the unique attribute that distinguishes voluntary from involuntary motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi proves that you can move voluntarily without willing it (as in absent-mindedness). Think of a moment today when you did something without consciously willing it—scratching an itch, shifting in your seat. Did that action come from you? If your will wasn't involved, what drove it? And if power is the real differentiator, how often do you exercise your power deliberately for Allah? Choose one routine action you will perform today with full presence and intention, as an act of worship using your God-given power.
?
If power is what distinguishes your voluntary acts from involuntary ones, then every intentional choice is a display of that attribute. Are you aware of your power in each action? Or do you let most of your day slip by on autopilot, missing the chance to consciously serve Allah through small, deliberate acts? What would change if you treated each voluntary motion as a gift of power to be used for His sake? Commit to a specific small action tomorrow (e.g., opening a door, smiling) and perform it with the explicit intention of obedience.
?
The Mu'tazila deny that your power has causal effect. Sanusi leaves that debate open. But what do you truly believe? Do you act as if your power independently produces results, or do you see it as a related attribute sustained by Allah? How would a deeper reliance on Allah change your approach to your daily tasks? Set a reminder today to pause before any action and silently say, 'This power is from Allah,' then act with trust.

Acts as Conventional Signs for Reward and Punishment
Sanusi explains why acts like trembling are conventional, not rational signs for reward/punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the connection between certain acts (like trembling) and reward/punishment is conventional (ja‘liyya) and not rational (‘aqliyya), and how this upholds God's absolute freedom in establishing signs.

Core Idea
God's signs for reward and punishment are conventional, not rationally necessary.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a potential objection: if involuntary acts like trembling are beyond human power, how can they be tied to reward or punishment? He explains that God has graciously removed obligation from such acts, but it would still be valid if He obligated them. The key insight is that the connection between an act and its consequence is purely conventional, established by divine will, not by rational necessity. Thus, God could assign any act as a sign for anything He wills.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
2
Identify the example and God's grace
Notice the example of trembling (ارتعاش). Sanusi says God has graciously removed the obligation regarding it. Why is this important? It shows God's freedom.
3
Understand the conditional argument
Sanusi says that even if God reversed the obligation or obligated all acts, it would be good because the creature's power has no effect. This reinforces that the basis is not human capability but divine will.
4
Grasp the conventional vs rational distinction
The final sentence is key: 'الدلالة في ذلك جعليَّة، لا عقليَّة'. The indication is conventional, set by the Law, not rationally inherent. This means God can designate any act as a sign for anything.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الارتعاش مثلاً - قد تفضَّلَ سبحانه بإسقاط التكليف به نفياً وإثباتاً ولو عكس سبحانَهُ التكليف ، أو كلَّفَ بالجميع .. لكان حسناً ؛ إذْ لا تأثير لقدرةِ المُكلَّف في الجميعِ ، وإنَّما تلك الأفعال المخلوقةُ اللهِ تعالى ، نصبَها الشرع عند اقترانها بأعراض حادثة كالقُدَرِ والإرادات . . أمارات على الثواب والعقاب ؛ فبالوجه الذي صح جعلُ بعض أفعالِهِ سبحانه عند اقترانِهِ بفعل لهُ آخرَ أمارةً على ما شاءَ مِنْ ثواب أو عقاب أو غيرهما.. صح جعله مجرَّداً عن غيره ، أو جعل غيره في مكانه أمارة على ذلكَ ؛ لأنَّ الدلالة في ذلك جعليَّةٌ ، لا عقليَّةٌ
Trembling, for example – He, exalted, has graciously removed the obligation concerning it, both in terms of affirmation and negation. If He, exalted, had reversed the obligation, or had obligated all acts, it would have been good; for the power of the obligated one has no effect in all [acts]. Rather, those created acts – God, exalted, [creates them] – the Law has set them up, when accompanied by accidental attributes such as capacities and wills, as signs for reward and punishment. So by the same consideration that it is valid for Him to make some of His acts, when accompanied by another act of His, a sign for whatever He wills of reward or punishment or other than them, it is valid to make it alone without the other, or to make another in its place a sign for that; because the indication in that is conventional, not rational.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God's acts, such as creating involuntary trembling, can serve as conventional signs for reward and punishment, not as rational or causal necessities. The author argues that God has graciously removed obligation from involuntary acts like trembling. If God had obligated them or reversed the obligation, it would still be good because the creature's power has no effect on these acts. Instead, the Law (shar‘) establishes created acts as signs (amārāt) for reward and punishment when they are accompanied by accidental attributes like capacities (qudar) and wills (irādāt). The key point is that this indication is conventional (ja‘liyya), not rational (‘aqliyya). Therefore, if God can make one act a sign when combined with another, He could also make that act alone a sign, or replace it with another, because there is no inherent rational necessity – it is entirely up to God's will and convention.

Key Terms
ارتعاش
تكليف
أمارات
ثواب
عقاب
جعليَّة
عقليَّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the connection between involuntary acts and reward/punishment is rationally necessary or inherent.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the indication is conventional, established by divine will and the Law, not by reason. Therefore, there is no rational necessity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it valid for God to make any act a sign for reward or punishment?
A) Because the act itself deserves reward or punishment.
B) Because the indication is conventional and depends on God's will.
C) Because human power creates the act.
D) Because reason demands it.

Key Takeaway
The connection between acts and reward/punishment is purely conventional, established by God's will, not by rational necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself feeling entitled to reward from God because of your good deeds, as if He owes you something? How does this lesson's emphasis on the conventional, not rational, nature of reward shake that assumption?
?
When you perform an act of worship, do you rely on the act itself or on God's mercy to accept it? This lesson shows that even the connection between obedience and reward is purely conventional. How will you change your mindset to one of total dependence on God's grace?
?
Consider an involuntary reaction you have, like fear or anger. Have you ever felt guilty for such reactions? How does knowing that God does not necessarily tie them to punishment free you from false guilt, and challenge you to trust His wise conventions?

The Comprehensiveness of Divine Will
Sanusi presents an alternative proof from the comprehensiveness of divine will against Qadariyya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the comprehensiveness of divine will serves as a proof that no action occurs independently of God's will, refuting the Qadariyya position.

Core Idea
God's will is as comprehensive as His power — nothing occurs without it.

Sanusi offers an alternative argument against the Qadariyya, who claimed that human actions can be produced by human power alone, independent of God's will. He draws an analogy from the comprehensiveness of God's power to argue for the comprehensiveness of His will. This lesson walks through that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the structure: an analogy is drawn between power and will.
2
Understand the analogy
The argument says: just as God's power attaches to every possible thing (making it feasible), so His will must attach to every possible thing (willing its existence or non-existence). Grasp this parallel.
3
Reflect on the refutation
Consider the conclusion: if an act occurs and God willed it, it happens. If someone else were to bring it about against God's will, that would imply God's will is ineffective, which is impossible. This disproves the Qadariyya claim.
The passage is cut off but the intended conclusion is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقرَّرَ المقترح هذا الدليل على وجه آخرَ ، وذلكَ أنْ قالَ : ( كما عم تعلُّق قدرتِهِ تعالى ؛ بمعنى : أنَّ كلَّ ممكن يتأَنَّى مِنَ الصانع فعله .. فكذلك لا بدَّ أنْ يريد وجودَهُ أو انتفاءه ؛ لعموم تعلقِ الإرادة ، فإذا كانَ الفعلُ معلوم الثبوتِ مثلاً .. وجبَ أنْ يكونَ مراداً ، وإذا قصد إلى إيقاعِهِ وأوقعَهُ غيرُهُ .. كانَ ذلكَ تحقيقاً لعدم نفوذ إرادته ونفوذ
And the proponent (al-muqtaḥ) formulated this proof in another way, and that is that he said: 'Just as the attachment of His power, the Exalted, is comprehensive, meaning that every possible act from the Maker is feasible, so too must He will its existence or non-existence, due to the comprehensiveness of the attachment of will. So if the act is, for example, known to be affirmative, then it must be willed. And if He intended to bring it about and someone else brings it about, then that constitutes a realization of the ineffectiveness of His will and the effectiveness...'
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative argument against the Qadariyya based on the comprehensiveness of divine will. The argument is that just as God's power extends to all possible things, so does His will encompass all things. Therefore, if an action occurs, it must be willed by God. If someone else were to bring about an action that God intended to bring about, that would imply that God's will is ineffective, which is impossible. Thus, human actions cannot occur independently of God's will, refuting the Qadariyya claim that human power can produce actions without divine will. The argument is cut off at the end, but the intended conclusion is clear.

Key Terms
إرادة
قدرة
نفوذ
تعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main premise of the alternative proof against Qadariyya?
A) God's power is limited.
B) God's will is comprehensive and thus every act must be willed by Him.
C) Human will overrides divine will.
D) Actions are purely accidental.

Key Takeaway
Because God's will is comprehensive, no action — including human actions — can occur without His willing it, refuting the Qadariyya position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that every action you take—every choice, every sin, every good deed—only occurs because God wills it, how would that change the way you view your own failures and successes? What would it mean for your sense of control?
?
Consider a time when you felt your own will was powerful enough to achieve something without God's help. How does this passage confront that assumption? What would it look like to genuinely surrender that belief?
?
If God's will is comprehensive and nothing occurs without it, then even your reading of this lesson is willed by Him. How does that make you feel? Does it give you comfort or unease? Why?

Necessary Attributes Entail Impossible Ones
Sanusi states the logical entailment of impossible from necessary attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical relationship between Allah's necessary attributes and their impossible opposites.

Core Idea
Every necessary attribute implies an impossible opposite.

Sanusi concludes his discussion of Allah's attributes with a powerful logical principle: once you know what must be true of Allah, you automatically know what cannot be true. This passage connects the positive attributes of perfection to their negations, forming a complete picture of divine nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the claim that knowing the necessary reveals the impossible.
The passage is short but dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the logical link
Notice the phrase 'ضد ذلك الواجب' — it shows that the impossible is the opposite of the necessary. This is a logical relation of contradiction.
For example, existence (necessary) and non-existence (impossible) are opposites.
3
Understand the significance
Reflect on why this principle matters. It means that all of Allah's negative attributes (what He cannot have) are derived from His positive attributes.
This avoids speculation: negative attributes are not arbitrary.
4
Check your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you can apply the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلُّهُ ممَّا هذا الذي ذُكِرَ في أوصافه تعالى إلى هنا هو يجبُ في حقِّهِ تعالى ، وإذا عُلِمَ ما يجبُ عُلِمَ ما يستحيلُ ؛ وهو الواجب ضدُّ ذلك الواجب
All of what has been mentioned of His attributes, exalted is He, up to here, is what is necessary in His right. And when what is necessary is known, what is impossible is known; and it is the necessary opposite of that necessary.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This is a concluding remark summarizing a preceding discussion on the divine attributes. The author states that everything mentioned up to this point concerning Allah's attributes is what is necessary (wājib) for Him. Then he introduces a foundational principle: knowing what is necessary for Allah necessarily entails knowing what is impossible for Him (mustaḥīl), because the impossible is simply the opposite of the necessary. The final phrase 'وهو الواجب ضد ذلك الواجب' (and it is the necessary opposite of that necessary) is somewhat ambiguous; it likely means that the impossible is the logical contrary of the necessary. In other words, each necessary attribute has a corresponding impossible opposite (e.g., existence vs. non-existence, knowledge vs. ignorance). This principle is a cornerstone of Islamic theology: the attributes of perfection (necessary) are known, and their negation (impossible) is derived by opposition.

Key Terms
واجب (wājib)
مستحيل (mustaḥīl)
ضد (ḍidd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That negative attributes (impossible) are independently known or not derived from positive ones.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly says that knowing the necessary entails knowing the impossible; they are opposites and logically linked.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does knowing what is necessary for Allah entail?
A) Knowing what is possible for Him.
B) Knowing what is impossible for Him.
C) Knowing nothing about Him.
D) Knowing His essence.

Key Takeaway
Knowing Allah's necessary attributes automatically reveals His impossible attributes because they are logical opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly knew that Allah is All-Knowing, would you ever act as if He is ignorant of your secrets? Search your heart: do you live as if Allah might not know what you do in private? How does that contradict the impossible attribute of ignorance?
?
The necessary attribute of power means weakness is impossible for Allah. In what areas of your life do you rely on your own strength rather than His? What does that say about your belief that He is All-Powerful and you are not?
?
Knowing that mercy is necessary for Allah implies that cruelty is impossible. When you face hardship, do you ever doubt His mercy? What would it mean to truly believe that cruelty is impossible for Him?

Proofs for the Permissibility of Seeing Allah
Sanusi enumerates four proofs for seeing Allah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four scriptural and rational proofs that establish the permissibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah is possible, but not through direction or facing.

Sanusi begins by stating the position that seeing Allah is permissible, then provides four proofs: the Quranic verse, Moses' request, the consensus of the Salaf, and multiple hadiths. He concludes that when such indicators accumulate, they yield certainty. This lesson examines each proof and the underlying principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the statement of permissibility
Notice that Sanusi affirms the possibility of seeing Allah 'with eyes' but immediately qualifies it: 'not in a direction nor facing.' This guards against anthropomorphism.
The qualification is crucial for correct understanding.
2
Examine the Quranic proof
Sanusi cites verse 75:23 ('Looking at their Lord'). Reflect on why this verse is a proof: it indicates that believers will see Allah in the afterlife.
This is a direct textual evidence.
3
Examine the proof from Moses and consensus
Sanusi argues that Moses' request to see Allah shows the possibility; a prophet would not ask for something impossible. Then he adds the consensus of the early Muslims who prayed to see Allah's face.
Both rational and historical evidence.
4
Understand the principle of multiple indications
Sanusi mentions the hadith 'You will see your Lord' and similar reports. He states that when many such apparent meanings (zawahir) gather, they produce certainty. This is a methodological point.
This principle is used to strengthen the proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل
فيما يجوز في حقه بنجانه في جواز رؤيته جل وعز
ويجوزُ في حقّهِ تعالى أنْ يُرى بالأبصارِ على ما يليقُ بهِ جل وعلا ، لا في جهةٍ ولا مقابلة ؛ لقوله تعالى : ﴿ إِلَى رَبِّهَا ناظرة ﴾ [القيامة : ۲۳] ، ولسؤال موسى عليه السلام لها ؛ إذْ لو كانت مستحيلةً ما جهل أمرها ، ولإجماع السلف الصالح قبل ظهورِ البدع على ابتهالهم إلى الله تعالى وطلبهم النظر إلى وجهه الكريم ، ولحديثِ : « سَتَرَوْنَ رَبَّكُمْ » ونحوِهِ ممَّا ورد ، والظواهر إذا كثُرَتْ في شيءٍ أَفادَتِ القطعَ بِهِ
لمَّا فرغَ مِنْ ذكرِ ما يجبُ في حقه تعالى وما يستحيل وهو مقابله . . شرع في ذكر ما يجوز (۱)
واعلم : أنَّهُ ليسَ المرادُ مِنْ هذا القسم رجوع الجواز إلى صفةٍ مِنْ صفاتِ ذاتِهِ ، تعالى عن ذلك ، بل إلى تعلقها بفعل مِنْ أَفعالِهِ جلَّ وعزَّ ؛ إذْ يستحيلُ أنْ يتصف سبحانه وتعالى بصفة جائزة ؛ لما عرفت
(۱) والجواز هنا : عقلي وشرعي معاً ، وهو أعم من الوقوع .
Chapter
On what is permissible in His right, [text: بنجانه] in the permissibility of seeing Him, glorified and exalted. And it is permissible in His right, exalted, that He be seen with eyes in a manner befitting Him, glorified and exalted, not in a direction nor facing, because of His saying: 'Looking at their Lord' [75:23], and because of the request of Moses, peace be upon him, for it; for if it were impossible, he would not have been ignorant of its matter, and because of the consensus of the righteous predecessors before the emergence of innovations on their supplication to Allah and their seeking the sight of His noble face, and because of the hadith: 'You will see your Lord' and similar narrations that have come, and when multiple indications (zawahir) abound concerning something, they yield certainty. When he finished mentioning what is necessary in His right and what is impossible, which is its counterpart, he began mentioning what is permissible. And know: that what is intended by this section is not the return of permissibility to an attribute of His essence—exalted is He above that—but rather to its connection with an action of His, glorified and exalted; for it is impossible that He, glorified and exalted, be described with a contingent (ja'iz) attribute, as you have learned. And the permissibility here is both rational and legal, and it is more general than occurrence.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage is a chapter (fasl) on the permissibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife. Sanusi first states the position: it is permissible to see Allah with the eyes in a manner befitting Him, without direction or facing (since He is not in a place). He gives four proofs: 1) The Quranic verse 'Looking at their Lord' (75:23) which the believers will do. 2) Moses asked to see Allah; if seeing were impossible, Moses would not have asked out of ignorance. 3) The consensus of the early Salaf who prayed for the vision of Allah's face. 4) The hadith 'You will see your Lord' and similar reports; when such multiple apparent meanings (zawahir) accumulate, they yield certainty. Then Sanusi clarifies that permissible here does not refer to a contingent attribute of Allah's essence (since He cannot have changing attributes), but rather to an action of His—that He may cause the sight to perceive Him. The permissibility is both rational (not impossible) and legal (confirmed by revelation), and it is more general than actual occurrence (i.e., it is possible even if it does not happen in this world).

Key Terms
رؤية
جواز
جهة
مقابلة
ظواهر
قطع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Seeing Allah would require direction and facing, like seeing a physical object.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly states it is 'not in a direction nor facing', meaning it is a vision without spatial limitations, befitting Allah's majesty.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the proofs Sanusi gives for the permissibility of seeing Allah?
A) The Quranic verse 'Looking at their Lord'
B) The request of Moses to see Allah
C) The consensus of the Salaf before innovations
D) The principle that Allah cannot be seen under any circumstances

Key Takeaway
Four cumulative proofs from scripture, prophetic precedent, consensus, and multiple hadiths establish that seeing Allah is possible in the afterlife, without direction or facing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you genuinely long to see your Lord, or have worldly attachments dulled that desire? What specific step will you take today to increase that longing?
?
The righteous predecessors would supplicate to see Allah's face. When did you last sincerely ask Allah for that? What holds you back from making it a regular plea?
?
If you truly believed you will see Allah tomorrow, what would you change in your actions today? Write down one concrete change and commit to it.

Clarifying the Nature of Permissibility
Sanusi clarifies the meaning of permissibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the permissibility of seeing Allah refers to a contingent possibility in His actions, not a change in His essence.

Core Idea
Permissibility in Allah pertains to His action, not His essence.

After presenting the proofs, Sanusi clarifies an important subtlety: 'permissible' (ja'iz) when applied to Allah does not mean He could possess a changeable attribute, but rather that He may cause the sight to perceive Him. He also notes that this permissibility is both rational and legal, and is broader than actual occurrence. This lesson unpacks that clarification.

Guided Path
1
Read the clarification text
Sanusi explains that the permissibility does not refer to an attribute of Allah's essence, but to His actions. Understand this distinction clearly.
Allah's attributes are necessary, not contingent.
2
Distinguish essence from action
Sanusi says it is impossible for Allah to have a 'ja'iz' (contingent) attribute. Instead, the permissibility lies in the action of causing vision. Ask yourself: what does this safeguard against?
This prevents imagining change in Allah.
3
Understand the dual nature of permissibility
Sanusi states that permissibility here is both rational (aqlī) and legal (shar‘ī), and it is more general than actual occurrence (wuqū‘). Consider why this distinction matters.
Something can be permissible without actually happening.

لمَّا فرغَ مِنْ ذكرِ ما يجبُ في حقه تعالى وما يستحيل وهو مقابله . . شرع في ذكر ما يجوز (۱)

Key Takeaway

?

?

?

How can you trust in Allah's wisdom that He does what He wills, not what we expect? What specific situation in your life do you need to surrender to His permission rather than your expectation?

The Permissibility of Seeing Allah and Its Four Evidences
Sanusi states the permissibility of seeing Allah and provides four proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that seeing Allah (ru'ya) is permissible in a manner befitting Him, and you will know the four textual proofs cited by Sanusi.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah is possible in the Hereafter; four proofs confirm it.

Sanusi begins by affirming that Allah can be seen by the believers in the Hereafter, in a way that suits His majesty, without direction or physical facing. He then marshals four key evidences from the Qur’an, Sunnah, and consensus. This lesson unpacks each proof and the principle that multiple apparent texts can yield certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the careful wording 'لا في جهة ولا مقابلة' (not in a direction nor facing).
2
Identify the four proofs
Find each of the four evidences Sanusi presents. They are introduced with 'لقوله تعالى', 'ولسؤال موسى', 'ولإجماع السلف', 'ولحديث'.
3
Reflect on the concluding principle
Consider the final sentence: 'والظواهر إذا كثرت في شيء أفادت القطع به.' How does this principle support the doctrine?
4
Check your understanding with the quiz
Answer the multiple-choice question to test your grasp of the evidences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل فيما يجوز في حقه سبحانه في جواز رؤيته جل وعز

ويجوز في حقه تعالى أن يرى بالأبصار على ما يليق به جل وعلا، لا في جهة ولا مقابلة؛ لقوله تعالى: {إِلَى رَبِّهَا ناظرة} [القيامة: 23]، ولسؤال موسى عليه السلام لها؛ إذ لو كانت مستحيلة ما جهل أمرها، ولإجماع السلف الصالح قبل ظهور البدع على ابتهالهم إلى الله تعالى وطلبهم النظر إلى وجهه الكريم، ولحديث: «ستَرَوْنَ رَبَّكُمْ» ونحوه مما ورد، والظواهر إذا كثرت في شيء أفادت القطع به.
Chapter on what is permissible with respect to Him, exalted is He, regarding the permissibility of seeing Him, mighty and majestic.

It is permissible with respect to Him, exalted is He, to be seen by the eyes in a manner befitting Him, mighty and sublime, not in a direction nor facing [Him]—due to His statement, exalted is He: {Some faces that Day will be radiant, looking at their Lord} [al-Qiyamah: 23], and due to Moses' request, peace be upon him, for it; for if it were impossible, he would not have been ignorant of its matter, and due to the consensus of the righteous predecessors before the emergence of innovations, that they would supplicate to Allah, exalted is He, and ask for looking at His noble face, and due to the hadith: 'You will see your Lord' and similar reports that have come. When numerous apparent [textual] indications [converge] on something, they yield certainty about it.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage is a statement of the doctrine that seeing Allah (ru'ya) is permissible (ja'iz) with respect to Allah, meaning it is possible for Him to be seen in the Hereafter in a manner befitting His majesty, without direction or facing (since He is not in a place). The author then provides four proofs:

1. The Qur'anic verse: {وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة} (Faces that Day will be radiant, looking at their Lord). The word 'nazira' (looking) implies sight.

2. The request of Prophet Musa (Moses) to see Allah, as mentioned in the Qur'an. If seeing Allah were impossible, Musa would have known that and would not have asked.

3. The consensus (ijma') of the early righteous predecessors (salaf) before the emergence of innovations, who would supplicate and ask to see Allah's noble face. This indicates they believed it was possible.

4. The prophetic hadith: 'ستَرَوْنَ رَبَّكُمْ' (You will see your Lord) and similar reports.

The author concludes that when numerous apparent textual evidences (zawahir) converge on a matter, they yield certainty (qat'). This is a principle of Islamic legal theory: multiple mutawatir-like indications can produce certainty.

Key Terms
رؤية (ru'ya)
جواز (jawaz)
نظر (nazar)
ظواهر (zawahir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Seeing Allah implies He is in a direction or has a physical form.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies 'لا في جهة ولا مقابلة' (not in a direction nor facing), meaning He is seen without spatial location or physical confrontation.

Key Takeaway

The Permissibility of Seeing Allah: An Act, Not an Attribute
Sanusi clarifies that the permissibility of ru'ya pertains to an act of Allah, not an attribute of His essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the permissibility (jawaz) of seeing Allah (ru'ya) refers to a possible act of Allah, not a contingent attribute of His essence.

Core Idea
Ru'ya is possible as an act of Allah, not as a description of His essence.

Sanusi has just finished explaining what is wajib (necessary) and mustahil (impossible) for Allah. Now he turns to the category of ja'iz (permissible). But here he needs to be precise: the 'permissibility' of the vision of Allah does not mean that Allah has a contingent attribute. Rather, it means that Allah may choose to create the vision for the believers. This distinction is crucial for preserving tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the full literal translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Pay attention to the footnote at the end.
2
Understand the context: after wajib and mustahil comes ja'iz
Sanusi positions this discussion as the third rational category after the necessary and impossible. Recognize that this is part of a systematic exposition.
3
Note the key clarification: ja'iz does not apply to Allah's essence
Sanusi explicitly states that the permissibility does not return to an attribute of Allah's essence. This is the main point of the lesson.
If it did, Allah would be described with contingent attributes (hawadith), which is impossible.
4
Understand the positive meaning: ru'ya is a possible act of Allah
The permissibility means that Allah's power may create the vision for His creation, or may not. It is neither necessary nor impossible.
The footnote adds that this permissibility is both rational and legal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لما فرغ من ذكر ما يجب في حقه تعالى وما يستحيل وهو مقابله.. شرع في ذكر ما يجوز(1)

واعلم: أنه ليس المراد من هذا القسم رجوع الجواز إلى صفة من صفات ذاته، تعالى عن ذلك، بل إلى تعلقها بفعل من أفعاله جل وعز؛ إذ يستحيل أن يتصف سبحانه وتعالى بصفة جائزة؛ لما عرفت من وجوب الوجود لذاته وجميع صفاته، ولو اتصف تعالى بجائز لكان متصفاً بالحوادث؛ إذ الجائز لا يكون إلا حادثاً، ويتعالى سبحانه ذلك وتعالى عن

وإذا عرفت هذا: فمعنى كون الرؤية جائزة في حقه تعالى أنه يجوز أن تتعلق قدرته تعالى بإيجادها لخلقه فيخلقها لهم على وفق مراده، ويجوز ألا يخلقها تعالى لهم، لا يستحيل في حقه تعالى خلقها ولا يجب.

(1) والجواز هنا: عقلي وشرعي معاً، وهو أعم من الوقوع.
When he finished mentioning what is obligatory (wajib) with respect to Him, exalted is He, and what is impossible (mustahil), and what is their counterpart, he began mentioning what is permissible (ja'iz). (1) And know that what is meant by this category is not that the permissibility returns to an attribute of His essence—exalted is He above that—but rather [it is a permissibility] related to an act of His, mighty and majestic; for it is impossible that He, glorified and exalted, be described with a contingent attribute, as you have understood from the necessity of existence for His essence and all His attributes. If He were described with a contingent [attribute], He would be described with accidents (hawadith), since the contingent is nothing but an accident. Exalted is He above that.

And when you understand this: the meaning of the vision (ru'ya) being permissible with respect to Him, exalted is He, is that His power, exalted is He, may be related to bringing it about for His creation—He creates it for them in accordance with His will—and it is also permissible that He not create it for them. It is not impossible with respect to Him, exalted is He, to create it, nor is it obligatory.

(1) And permissibility here: both rational (aqli) and legal (shar'i) together, and it is more general than actual occurrence.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage clarifies what is meant by saying that seeing Allah (ru'ya) is permissible (ja'iz). The author notes that after finishing the sections on what is obligatory (wajib) and impossible (mustahil) regarding Allah—which are opposites—he now addresses what is permissible.

He then makes a crucial point: the permissibility of ru'ya does not mean that some attribute of Allah's essence is contingent. That would be impossible because Allah's essence and attributes are necessary (wajib al-wujud). If any attribute were contingent, it would entail that Allah is subject to temporal accidents (hawadith), which is impossible.

Rather, the permissibility relates to Allah's act: He may create the vision for the believers in the Hereafter, or He may not. It is within His power to do so; it is not impossible (mustahil) nor obligatory (wajib). This is the meaning of 'ja'iz' in this context.

The footnote adds that this permissibility is both rational (aqli) and legal (shar'i), and it is more general than actual occurrence—that is, it does not imply that the vision will definitely happen, only that it is possible.

Key Terms
وجوب (wujub)
استحالة (istihala)
جواز (jawaz)
حادث (hadith)
صفة (sifa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that saying ru'ya is 'ja'iz' means that Allah has a contingent attribute.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly refutes this: ja'iz here does not apply to Allah's essence, but to His act of creating the vision.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why can't the permissibility of ru'ya be an attribute of Allah's essence?
A) Because Allah's essence is not known
B) Because Allah's essence is necessary and cannot be described with contingent attributes
C) Because ru'ya is impossible
D) Because the Salaf did not discuss it

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of seeing Allah is a possibility of an act, not a description of His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you anticipate seeing Allah on the Day of Judgment, do you think of it as a right you have earned or as a pure act of His generosity? How does this distinction change the way you hope for it?
?
The passage teaches that Allah's attributes are all necessary and perfect. Are there any attributes you implicitly assume are contingent or lacking in perfection? How does that affect your trust in Him?
?
If the vision of Allah is a created act, what does that imply about your relationship with Him in this life—are you actively seeking signs of His presence, or are you passively waiting for the Hereafter?

The Mu'tazila Objection and the Transmitted Proof for Ru'ya
Sanusi presents the Mu'tazila objection and the Ahl al-Sunna transmitted proof, analyzing the Qur'anic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ahl al-Sunna use linguistic analysis of a Qur'anic verse to defend the permissibility of seeing Allah, and how the Mu'tazila reinterpreted the verse.

Core Idea
A single preposition can change the meaning of revelation—language defends the vision of Allah.

The debate over seeing Allah in the afterlife is one of the most famous disputes between the Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna. The Mu'tazila considered the vision impossible, while the Sunnis argued it is possible. In this passage, Sanusi presents the Sunni case from the Qur'an, focusing on the linguistic meaning of the verb 'nazara' when used with the preposition 'ila'. He also shows how the Mu'tazili scholar al-Jubba'i tried to reinterpret the verse, and why his interpretation fails linguistically.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Note the structure: objection, proof, linguistic rule, counter-interpretation, refutation.
Pay attention to the footnotes—they contain crucial linguistic details.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila objection
Find the sentence where the Mu'tazila claim that Allah creating this vision is impossible. Understand their position.
The objection is stated very briefly.
3
Understand the Sunni transmitted proof
The Sunni proof begins with 'احتج أهل السنة على الجواز بالسمع والعقل' but only the transmitted part is given. Focus on the verse and the linguistic argument about 'nazara' with 'ila'.
The author uses the verb 'nazara' to argue for sight.
4
Analyze the linguistic rule
The footnotes explain the different meanings of 'nazara' with different prepositions. Use them to understand why 'nazara ila' must mean sight.
This is the key to the proof.
5
Examine al-Jubba'i's reinterpretation and its refutation
Al-Jubba'i claimed 'nazara' means waiting and 'ila' is a noun. See how the linguistic authorities refute this.
The quotation from al-Azhari is decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت المعتزلة: بل خلقه تعالى لهذه الرؤية مستحيل.

احتج أهل السنة على الجواز بالسمع والعقل:

أما السمع: فمنه قوله تعالى: {وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ * إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ} [القيامة: 22-23]، وذلك لأن النظر إذا تعدى بحرف (إلى) كان ظاهراً في معنى الرؤية(1)، ويؤكد أن المعني بهذا النظر الرؤية: إسناد هذا النظر إلى الوجه الذي هو محل العين الباصرة.

وحمل الجبائي النظر في الآية على معنى الانتظار(2)، وجعل (إلى) اسماً بمعنى النعمة، مفرد الآلاء مضافاً لما بعده، لا حرف.

(1) وإن تعدى فعل النظر بنفسه كان بمعنى الانتظار، أو ب(عن) فبمعنى التفكر، أو باللام كان بمعنى العطف؛ كقولك: نظرت له؛ أي: عطفت عليه.

(2) قال العلامة الأزهري في «تهذيب اللغة» (ن ظ ر) (266/14): (ومن قال: إن معنى قوله: {إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ} [القيامة: 23] بمعنى منتظرة.. فقد أخطأ؛ لأن العرب لا تقول: نظرت إلى الشيء؛ بمعنى: انتظرته، إنما تقول: نظرت فلاناً؛ أي: انتظرته).
The Mu'tazila said: Rather, His creating this vision is impossible.

The Ahl al-Sunna argued for its permissibility by transmitted (sam') and rational (aql) evidence:

As for the transmitted evidence: among it is His statement, exalted is He: {[Some] faces that Day will be radiant, looking at their Lord} [al-Qiyamah: 22-23]. That is because the verb 'nazara' (to look), when it is transitive by the particle 'ila' (to), is clearly in the sense of sight (ru'ya). (1) And it confirms that what is meant by this 'nazar' is sight: the attribution of this 'nazar' to the face, which is the locus of the seeing eye.

Al-Jubba'i interpreted 'nazar' in the verse as meaning 'waiting' (intizar), (2) and he made 'ila' a noun meaning 'favor' (ni'ma), the singular of 'ala' (blessings), annexed to what follows it, not a particle.

(1) And if the verb 'nazara' is transitive by itself, it means 'waiting'; or with 'an' it means 'reflecting'; or with 'lam' it means 'compassion'; as in your saying: 'nazartu lahu' meaning 'I showed compassion to him'.

(2) Al-Azhari said in 'Tahdhib al-Lugha' (n-z-r, 14/266): 'Whoever says that the meaning of His statement {إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ} [al-Qiyamah: 23] is "waiting" has erred; for the Arabs do not say "nazartu ila al-shay'" meaning "I waited for it". Rather, they say "nazarathu" meaning "I waited for him".'
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila objection and the Ahl al-Sunna response, focusing on the transmitted (sam'i) evidence. The author first states the Mu'tazila position: they claim that Allah creating the vision (ru'ya) for the believers is impossible (mustahil).

The Ahl al-Sunna then argue for its permissibility, dividing their proof into transmitted and rational (though only the transmitted is given in this passage). The transmitted evidence is the Qur'anic verse {وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة}. The linguistic argument is that when the verb 'nazara' is followed by the preposition 'ila', it indicates sight (ru'ya). This is reinforced by the fact that the 'nazar' is attributed to the face, which is the seat of the seeing eye.

The author then mentions the interpretation of al-Jubba'i (a Mu'tazili scholar) who reinterpreted the verse to mean 'waiting' (intizar) and took 'ila' as a noun meaning 'favor'. This is refuted by the linguistic evidence provided in the footnotes: The verb 'nazara' with 'ila' does not mean waiting; waiting is expressed by 'nazarahu' (direct object) or 'nazara lahu'? Actually the footnote explains the different transitivity patterns. The first footnote clarifies the meanings of 'nazara' with different prepositions: by itself (direct object) = waiting; with 'an' = reflecting; with 'lam' = compassion. So 'nazara ila' must mean sight, not waiting. The second footnote quotes al-Azhari, a renowned linguist, explicitly refuting the interpretation that 'nazara ila' means waiting.

Thus, the Ahl al-Sunna defend the literal meaning of the verse as proof for the permissibility of seeing Allah.

Key Terms
رؤية
نظر
معتزلة
جبائي
سمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'nazara ila' in the Qur'an can mean 'waiting for' something.
Correction: The verb 'nazara' when followed by 'ila' indicates sight (ru'ya), as confirmed by linguistic analysis and the attribution to the face.

Checkpoint
How did al-Jubba'i reinterpret the word 'nazar' in the verse?
A) As 'reflection' (tafakkur)
B) As 'compassion' (atf)
C) As 'waiting' (intizar)
D) As 'hearing' (sama')

Key Takeaway
The transmission evidence for the permissibility of seeing Allah is based on a precise linguistic understanding of the Qur'anic verse {وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة}.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse of the Qur'an that challenges your current understanding, do you immediately look for alternative interpretations to make it comfortable, or do you humble yourself before its plain meaning as the Arabs understood it? This lesson shows that a wrong linguistic choice led to a major theological error. Are there verses in your life where you have subtly reinterpreted Allah's words to suit your desires?
?
The Mu'tazila rejected the vision of Allah because they considered it impossible for a non-physical being. Is there an attribute of Allah that you struggle to accept because it does not fit your limited reasoning? Will you submit to the text even when it strains your intellect, trusting that Allah knows better than you?
?
Al-Jubba'i changed the meaning of a single preposition ('ila') to avoid a belief he found problematic. What theological belief do you find uncomfortable, and how might you be tempted to twist the clear words of Allah or His Messenger to escape it? Repent from that tendency now, and resolve to accept the truth even when it is difficult.
