Cumulative Scriptural Evidence for the Vision of Allah
Sanusi defends certainty of vision through cumulative scriptural evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how multiple apparent (ẓāhir) scriptural proofs together produce certainty (qaṭʿ) about the vision of Allah.

Core Idea
Many apparent proofs agreeing on one meaning yield certainty.

Sanusi addresses a key challenge: the scriptural evidences for the vision of Allah are each only apparent (ẓāhir), not explicit texts (naṣṣ). How then can they be conclusive? He argues that their abundance and unanimous agreement on the same meaning transforms them into certain proof. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows how even one individual proof, like Moses' request, is nearly explicit.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the initial clarification about likening vision to vision, not seen to seen.
The first sentence sets a boundary: direction and corporeality are impossible for Allah.
2
Find the core claim
Locate the sentence where Sanusi states that many apparent proofs, due to their abundance and agreement, yield certainty. This is the main argument.
It begins with 'وهذه الأدلة ونحوها...'
3
Understand the refutation of al-Rāzī
Sanusi mentions Ibn al-Tilimsānī's refutation of Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī. al-Rāzī doubted certainty because of lack of rational proof and considered scriptural proofs not explicit. See how the cumulative argument answers this.
4
Examine the strongest individual evidence
Sanusi points out that some individual proofs, like Moses' request (Quran 7:143) and the hadith 'You will see your Lord', are almost explicit texts. Reflect on how they support the cumulative case.
The hadith is widely transmitted (mustafīḍ) and accepted by the imams.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقت الرؤية، أما الجهة والجسمية ولوازمهما فمستحيلة في حقه تعالى، وبالجملة: فالمقصود تشبيه الرؤية بالرؤية فيما ذكر، لا المرئي بالمرئي. وهذه الأدلة ونحوها من أدلة السمع وإن كان كل واحد منها ظاهراً ليس بنص... فهي لكثرتها وتواطئها على معنى واحد تفيد القطع بالرؤية، وإلى هذا المعنى أشرت بقولي: (والظواهر إذا كثرت...) إلى آخره. وقد أشار إلى هذا المعنى شرف الدين بن التلمساني رحمه الله تعالى رداً على الإمام الفخر في ميله إلى عدم القطع بجواز الرؤية لما لم يتضح له الدليل العقلي عليها (1)، والأدلة السمعية رآها ليست بنص، فرد عليه بما سبق؛ وهو ظاهر، على أن بعض تلك الأدلة على الانفراد - كسؤال موسى عليه السلام للرؤية - يكاد أن يكون نصاً في جواز الرؤية (3)، ويقرب منه حديث: «سترون ربكم، فإنه نص» (1)، وهو حديث مستفيض تلقته الأئمة بالقبول.
At the time of vision, as for direction and corporeality and their necessities, they are impossible in His right, Exalted is He. In summary: the purpose is to liken vision to vision in what was mentioned, not the seen to the seen. And these evidences and similar from the evidences of revelation, even if each one of them is apparent (ẓāhir) not an explicit text (naṣṣ)... yet because of their abundance and their agreement on one meaning, they yield certainty (qaṭʿ) regarding the vision. And to this meaning I alluded by my saying: 'And the apparent proofs when they become abundant...' to its end. And Sharaf al-Dīn ibn al-Tilimsānī, may Allah have mercy on him, alluded to this meaning in refutation of al-Imām al-Fakhr in his inclination to not being certain of the permissibility of vision since the rational proof for it was not clear to him (1), and he saw that the scriptural evidences were not explicit texts, so he refuted him with what preceded; and it is apparent. Moreover, some of those individual evidences – such as the request of Moses, peace be upon him, for vision – are almost an explicit text (naṣṣ) regarding the permissibility of vision (3), and close to it is the hadith: 'You will see your Lord, for it is an explicit text' (1), and it is a widely transmitted (mustafīḍ) hadith which the imams have accepted.
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the proof for the vision of Allah (ru'ya) based on cumulative scriptural evidence. Sanusi begins by clarifying that the comparison is between the act of seeing in this world and the act of seeing in the afterlife, not between the seen objects. He then argues that although each individual proof from revelation is only apparent (zāhir) and not an explicit, unambiguous text (naṣṣ), the sheer number of such proofs and their agreement on the same meaning—namely, that believers will see Allah—produces certainty (qaṭʿ). He refers to his own earlier statement about 'al-ẓawāhir idhā kathurat' (apparent proofs when they are numerous). Next, he mentions that Sharaf al-Dīn ibn al-Tilimsānī used this same argument to refute Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī, who was reluctant to affirm certainty about the vision because he found no clear rational proof and considered the scriptural evidences not explicit. Sanusi then strengthens his case by noting that some individual proofs, such as Moses' request to see Allah (Quran 7:143), are almost explicit, and the prophetic hadith 'You will see your Lord' is explicit (naṣṣ) and widely transmitted (mustafīḍ), accepted by the scholars without objection. Thus, the multitude of proofs compensates for any individual weakness and yields definitive knowledge.

Key Terms
رؤية
ظاهر
نص
قطعي
مستفيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Each scriptural proof must be an explicit text (naṣṣ) to prove the vision of Allah.
Correction: Sanusi shows that even if each individual proof is only apparent (ẓāhir), their abundance and agreement produce certainty (qaṭʿ).

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why do multiple apparent (ẓāhir) scriptural proofs yield certainty about the vision of Allah?
A) Because each proof is individually explicit (naṣṣ).
B) Because they are numerous and agree on one meaning.
C) Because rational proofs are also available.
D) Because the Mu'tazila accepted them.

Key Takeaway
The abundance and agreement of apparent scriptural proofs produce certainty about the vision of Allah, even if each alone is not explicit.

Refutation of Quranic Objections to the Vision of Allah
Sanusi refutes two Quranic objections to the vision of Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes two Quranic verses used by the Mu'tazila to deny the vision of Allah in the afterlife.

Core Idea
Quranic verses that seem to deny seeing Allah are actually limited to this world or to comprehensive grasp, not vision itself.

Sanusi now addresses two key Quranic objections raised by the Mu'tazila against the possibility of seeing Allah. These verses appear to negate vision entirely, but careful analysis reveals that they are either speaking about a different concept or are restricted to this world. This lesson will guide you through Sanusi's precise refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the two objections and Sanusi's responses.
Pay attention to the key terms like idrāk and ru'ya.
2
Understand the first refutation
Sanusi explains that ‘No vision can grasp Him’ does not negate vision because ‘grasp’ (idrāk) means comprehensive encompassing, not mere sight.
3
Understand the second refutation
The verse ‘You shall not see Me’ is a response to Moses' request in this world, so it does not apply to the afterlife.
The logical principle about negation of temporal statements is key.
4
Review the logical argument
Consider how Sanusi uses the principle from logic that the negation of a time-bound statement is understood within that time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا معارضة بقوله تعالى: ﴿لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ﴾ [الأنعام: 103] ولا بقوله جل وعز: ﴿لَنْ تَرَانِي﴾ [الأعراف: 143]. فلا يعارضها قوله تعالى: ﴿لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ﴾ لأن الإدراك أخص لإشعاره بالإحاطة، ولا شك أنها منفية مطلقاً. سلمنا أنها الرؤية، لكن المراد في الدنيا، أو هو من باب الكل لا الكلية. ولا قوله جل وعز: ﴿لَنْ تَرَانِي﴾ لأن المراد في الدنيا؛ إذ هو المسؤول لموسى عليه السلام، والأصل في الجواب المطابقة، ولهذا قال: ﴿لَنْ تَرَانِي﴾ ولم يقل: لن أرى، أو: لن تمكن رؤيتي، وقد يستأنس لذلك بما تقرر في المنطق: أن نقيض الوقتية يؤخذ فيه وقتها المعين. هذا مما استدل به المعتزلة من السمع على استحالة الرؤية.
There is no opposition by His statement, Exalted is He: 'No vision can grasp Him' (Quran 6:103), nor by His statement, Mighty and Majestic: 'You shall not see Me' (Quran 7:143). For His statement 'No vision can grasp Him' does not oppose them because 'comprehension' (idrāk) is more specific, indicating encompassing, and there is no doubt that it is negated absolutely. Even if we grant that it means vision, then the intended meaning is in this world, or it is a case of the whole but not the universality. And His statement, Mighty and Majestic: 'You shall not see Me' does not oppose because the intended meaning is in this world, since it was the request of Moses, peace be upon him, and the principle in a response is correspondence; therefore He said 'You shall not see Me' and did not say 'I will not be seen' or 'My vision is not possible'. And one may seek support for that from what is established in logic: that the negation of a temporal statement is taken with its specific time. This is among the scriptural evidences with which the Mu'tazila argued for the impossibility of vision.
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two Quranic verses that the Mu'tazila use to deny the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife. First, Sanusi addresses 'No vision can grasp Him' (Quran 6:103). He argues that this verse does not oppose the proofs for vision because 'idrāk' (comprehensive grasp or encompassing) is more specific than mere 'ru'ya' (vision). The negation of comprehensive grasp does not negate vision, as one can see without fully encompassing. Moreover, the negation of idrāk is absolute and applies in this life and the next, but that does not preclude simple vision. Then he concedes hypothetically that 'idrāk' might mean 'ru'ya', but even then the verse refers to this world, or it is a negation of the whole category without negating every individual instance (the principle of 'al-kull lā al-kulliyya'). Second, he addresses 'You shall not see Me' (Quran 7:143), which was said to Moses. Sanusi argues that this response is specific to this world and to Moses' request at that time. The principle is that a response matches the request, and since Moses asked to see Allah in this world, the answer was 'You shall not see Me in this world'. The verse does not negate vision in the afterlife. He also invokes a logical principle: the negation of a temporal proposition is understood within that specific time. Finally, he notes that this is the scriptural argument used by the Mu'tazila, but he has refuted it by proper contextualization and distinction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Quran categorically denies any possibility of seeing Allah.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the verses are either about comprehensive grasp (not simple vision) or are restricted to this world.

Checkpoint
How does Sanusi refute the objection based on Quran 6:103 ('No vision can grasp Him')?
A) He accepts that the verse negates vision altogether.
B) He argues that 'idrāk' is comprehensive grasp, which is negated, not simple vision.
C) He says the verse was abrogated.
D) He claims the verse refers only to the Prophet Muhammad.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes Quranic objections by showing that they either refer to comprehensive grasp (not simple vision) or are limited to this world, thus allowing the vision of Allah in the afterlife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever read a Quranic verse and let it shake your conviction about a core belief, without first seeking deeper understanding? How does Sanusi's careful distinction between idrak and ru'ya challenge you to be more humble and scholarly in your approach to revelation? What will you do when you next encounter a verse that seems to contradict what you know?
?
The Mu'tazila held firmly to their interpretation, yet Sanusi shows that they missed crucial nuances. Reflect on your own beliefs: is there a teaching you hold that might be based on a superficial reading of a text? What would it take for you to re-examine that belief with the same rigor Sanusi applies here?
?
When you pray for something, or when God answers a request, do you assume that the answer is absolute and everlasting? Moses asked for vision in this world, and God replied 'You shall not see Me'—limited to that moment. How might this change how you understand God's responses to your own prayers and requests? What does this teach you about God's wisdom and the importance of context?
